# Description of the Dataset
## General Description
The dataset includes diabetes related factors extracted from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS), year 2015.
The original BRFSS, from which this dataset is derived, is a health-related telephone survey that is collected annually by the CDC.
Each year, the survey collects responses from over 400,000 Americans on health-related risk behaviors, chronic health conditions, and the use of
preventative services. These features are either questions directly asked of participants, or calculated variables based on individual participant
responses.

## Data Files
The dataset consists of 1 data file:

### "diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv"
The csv file is a clean dataset of 253,680 responses (rows) and 22 features (columns).
All rows with missing values were removed from the original dataset; the current file contains no missing values.

The columns in the dataset are:

#1 `Diabetes_binary`: (int, bool) Diabetes (0=no, 1=yes)
#2 `HighBP`: (int, bool) High Blood Pressure (0=no, 1=yes)
#3 `HighChol`: (int, bool) High Cholesterol (0=no, 1=yes)
#4 `CholCheck`: (int, bool) Cholesterol check in 5 years (0=no, 1=yes)
#5 `BMI`: (int, numerical) Body Mass Index
#6 `Smoker`: (int, bool) (0=no, 1=yes)
#7 `Stroke`: (int, bool) Stroke (0=no, 1=yes)
#8 `HeartDiseaseorAttack`: (int, bool) coronary heart disease (CHD) or myocardial infarction (MI), (0=no, 1=yes)
#9 `PhysActivity`: (int, bool) Physical Activity in past 30 days (0=no, 1=yes)
#10 `Fruits`: (int, bool) Consume one fruit or more each day (0=no, 1=yes)
#11 `Veggies`: (int, bool) Consume one Vegetable or more each day (0=no, 1=yes)
#12 `HvyAlcoholConsump` (int, bool) Heavy drinkers (0=no, 1=yes)
#13 `AnyHealthcare` (int, bool) Have any kind of health care coverage (0=no, 1=yes)
#14 `NoDocbcCost` (int, bool) Was there a time in the past 12 months when you needed to see a doctor but could not because of cost? (0=no, 1=yes)
#15 `GenHlth` (int, ordinal) self-reported health (1=excellent, 2=very good, 3=good, 4=fair, 5=poor)
#16 `MentHlth` (int, ordinal) How many days during the past 30 days was your mental health not good? (1 - 30 days)
#17 `PhysHlth` (int, ordinal) Hor how many days during the past 30 days was your physical health not good? (1 - 30 days)
#18 `DiffWalk` (int, bool) Do you have serious difficulty walking or climbing stairs? (0=no, 1=yes)
#19 `Sex` (int, categorical) Sex (0=female, 1=male)
#20 `Age` (int, ordinal) Age, 13-level age category in intervals of 5 years (1= 18 - 24, 2= 25 - 29, ..., 12= 75 - 79, 13 = 80 or older)
#21 `Education` (int, ordinal) Education level on a scale of 1 - 6 (1=Never attended school, 2=Elementary, 3=Some high school, 4=High school, 5=Some
college, 6=College)
#22 `Income` (int, ordinal) Income scale on a scale of 1 to 8 (1= <=10K, 2= <=15K, 3= <=20K, 4= <=25K, 5= <=35K, 6= <=50K, 7= <=75K, 8= >75K)

Sure, here is a python screenplay


import pandas as pd

# Load the data
data = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# Open the output file
with open('data_exploration.txt', 'w') as f:

    # Data Size
    f.write('# Data Size\n')
    f.write('Number of rows: {}\n'.format(data.shape[0]))
    f.write('Number of columns: {}\n'.format(data.shape[1]))
    f.write('\n')

    # Summary Statistics
    f.write('# Summary Statistics\n')
    f.write(str(data.describe()))
    f.write('\n\n')

    # Categorical Variables
    f.write('# Categorical Variables\n')
    categorical_cols = data.select_dtypes(include=['object']).columns
    for col in categorical_cols:
        f.write('Column Name: {}\n'.format(col))
        f.write('Most Common Value: {}\n'.format(data[col].mode()[0]))
    if categorical_cols.size == 0:
        f.write('Not Applicable\n')
    f.write('\n')

    # Missing Values
    f.write('# Missing Values\n')
    missing_values = data.isnull().sum()
    n_missing_values = missing_values.sum()
    if n_missing_values == 0:
        f.write('No missing values')
    else:
        f.write(str(missing_values))
    f.write('\n\n')

[6] ===== SURROGATE {Performer} -> "Data Exploration Code"  ==========================================================================================
Here is the code to perform the analysis.
It creates the file "data_exploration.txt".


import pandas as pd

# Load the data
data = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# Open the output file
with open('data_exploration.txt', 'w') as f:

    # Data Size
    f.write('# Data Size\n')
    f.write('Number of rows: {}\n'.format(data.shape[0]))
    f.write('Number of columns: {}\n'.format(data.shape[1]))
    f.write('\n')

    # Summary Statistics
    f.write('# Summary Statistics\n')
    f.write(str(data.describe()))
    f.write('\n\n')

    # Categorical Variables
    f.write('# Categorical Variables\n')
    categorical_cols = data.select_dtypes(include=['object']).columns
    for col in categorical_cols:
        f.write('Column Name: {}\n'.format(col))
        f.write('Most Common Value: {}\n'.format(data[col].mode()[0]))
    if categorical_cols.size == 0:
        f.write('Not Applicable\n')
    f.write('\n')

    # Missing Values
    f.write('# Missing Values\n')
    missing_values = data.isnull().sum()
    n_missing_values = missing_values.sum()
    if n_missing_values == 0:
        f.write('No missing values')
    else:
        f.write(str(missing_values))
    f.write('\n\n')

preventative services. These features are either questions directly asked of participants, or calculated variables based on individual participant
responses.

# Data Exploration Code
Here is our Data Exploration Code:


import pandas as pd

# Load the data
data = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# Open the output file
with open('data_exploration.txt', 'w') as f:

    # Data Size
    f.write('# Data Size\n')
    f.write('Number of rows: {}\n'.format(data.shape[0]))
    f.write('Number of columns: {}\n'.format(data.shape[1]))
    f.write('\n')

    # Summary Statistics
    f.write('# Summary Statistics\n')
    f.write(str(data.describe()))
    f.write('\n\n')

    # Categorical Variables
    f.write('# Categorical Variables\n')
    categorical_cols = data.select_dtypes(include=['object']).columns
    for col in categorical_cols:
        f.write('Column Name: {}\n'.format(col))
        f.write('Most Common Value: {}\n'.format(data[col].mode()[0]))
    if categorical_cols.size == 0:
        f.write('Not Applicable\n')
    f.write('\n')

    # Missing Values
    f.write('# Missing Values\n')
    missing_values = data.isnull().sum()
    n_missing_values = missing_values.sum()
    if n_missing_values == 0:
        f.write('No missing values')
    else:
        f.write(str(missing_values))
    f.write('\n\n')

# Description of the Original Dataset
## General Description
The dataset includes diabetes related factors extracted from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS), year 2015.
The original BRFSS, from which this dataset is derived, is a health-related telephone survey that is collected annually by the CDC.
Each year, the survey collects responses from over 400,000 Americans on health-related risk behaviors, chronic health conditions, and the use of
preventative services. These features are either questions directly asked of participants, or calculated variables based on individual participant
responses.

## Data Files
The dataset consists of 1 data file:

### "diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv"
The csv file is a clean dataset of 253,680 responses (rows) and 22 features (columns).
All rows with missing values were removed from the original dataset; the current file contains no missing values.

The columns in the dataset are:

#1 `Diabetes_binary`: (int, bool) Diabetes (0=no, 1=yes)
#2 `HighBP`: (int, bool) High Blood Pressure (0=no, 1=yes)
#3 `HighChol`: (int, bool) High Cholesterol (0=no, 1=yes)
#4 `CholCheck`: (int, bool) Cholesterol check in 5 years (0=no, 1=yes)
#5 `BMI`: (int, numerical) Body Mass Index
#6 `Smoker`: (int, bool) (0=no, 1=yes)
#7 `Stroke`: (int, bool) Stroke (0=no, 1=yes)
#8 `HeartDiseaseorAttack`: (int, bool) coronary heart disease (CHD) or myocardial infarction (MI), (0=no, 1=yes)
#9 `PhysActivity`: (int, bool) Physical Activity in past 30 days (0=no, 1=yes)
#10 `Fruits`: (int, bool) Consume one fruit or more each day (0=no, 1=yes)
#11 `Veggies`: (int, bool) Consume one Vegetable or more each day (0=no, 1=yes)
#12 `HvyAlcoholConsump` (int, bool) Heavy drinkers (0=no, 1=yes)
#13 `AnyHealthcare` (int, bool) Have any kind of health care coverage (0=no, 1=yes)
#14 `NoDocbcCost` (int, bool) Was there a time in the past 12 months when you needed to see a doctor but could not because of cost? (0=no, 1=yes)
#15 `GenHlth` (int, ordinal) self-reported health (1=excellent, 2=very good, 3=good, 4=fair, 5=poor)
#16 `MentHlth` (int, ordinal) How many days during the past 30 days was your mental health not good? (1 - 30 days)
#17 `PhysHlth` (int, ordinal) Hor how many days during the past 30 days was your physical health not good? (1 - 30 days)
#18 `DiffWalk` (int, bool) Do you have serious difficulty walking or climbing stairs? (0=no, 1=yes)
#19 `Sex` (int, categorical) Sex (0=female, 1=male)
#20 `Age` (int, ordinal) Age, 13-level age category in intervals of 5 years (1= 18 - 24, 2= 25 - 29, ..., 12= 75 - 79, 13 = 80 or older)
#21 `Education` (int, ordinal) Education level on a scale of 1 - 6 (1=Never attended school, 2=Elementary, 3=Some high school, 4=High school, 5=Some
college, 6=College)
#22 `Income` (int, ordinal) Income scale on a scale of 1 to 8 (1= <=10K, 2= <=15K, 3= <=20K, 4= <=25K, 5= <=35K, 6= <=50K, 7= <=75K, 8= >75K)
------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

# Data Exploration Code and Output
Here is our Data Exploration Code:


import pandas as pd

# Load the data
data = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# Open the output file
with open('data_exploration.txt', 'w') as f:

    # Data Size
    f.write('# Data Size\n')
    f.write('Number of rows: {}\n'.format(data.shape[0]))
    f.write('Number of columns: {}\n'.format(data.shape[1]))
    f.write('\n')

    # Summary Statistics
    f.write('# Summary Statistics\n')
    f.write(str(data.describe()))
    f.write('\n\n')

    # Categorical Variables
    f.write('# Categorical Variables\n')
    categorical_cols = data.select_dtypes(include=['object']).columns
    for col in categorical_cols:
        f.write('Column Name: {}\n'.format(col))
        f.write('Most Common Value: {}\n'.format(data[col].mode()[0]))
    if categorical_cols.size == 0:
        f.write('Not Applicable\n')
    f.write('\n')

    # Missing Values
    f.write('# Missing Values\n')
    missing_values = data.isnull().sum()
    n_missing_values = missing_values.sum()
    if n_missing_values == 0:
        f.write('No missing values')
    else:
        f.write(str(missing_values))
    f.write('\n\n')

- "ISSUES":
The keys of this dictionary should briefly describe the statistical issues that we should account for.
The values should describe the issue and how it should be accounted for in the statistical tests.
For possible issues (keys), consider for example:
* Confounding variables (see available variables in the dataset that we can adjust for).
* Missing data points.
* Dependencies between data points.
* Multiple comparisons.
* Imbalanced data.
* Any other relevant statistical issues.

- Hypotheses.
The keys of this dictionary should briefly describe each of our hypotheses.
The values of this dictionary
should specify the most adequate statistical test for each hypothesis, and describe how it should be performed while accounting for any issues you
have outlined above.

For each of our hypotheses, suggest a *single* statistical test.
If there are several possible ways to test a given hypothesis, specify only *one* statistical test (the simplest one).

Cholesterol), Stroke. We will perform separate logistic regression models to test the association of each key variable with diabetes, with

variable and each of the variables mentioned as the dependent variables. These models will include control variables such as Age, BMI, and Sex to

[13] ==== ASSISTANT{'model_engine': <ModelEngine.GPT4: 'gpt-4'>} {Performer} -> "Data Analysis Code"  ================================================
Here is the Python code to perform the described data analysis.


# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS

## DF 1: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP", data=df).fit()
df1 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## DF 2: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighChol, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighChol", data=df).fit()
df2 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

## DF 3: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against Stroke, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ Stroke", data=df).fit()
df3 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df3.to_pickle('df_3.pkl')

## DF 4: "Multivariable logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, HighChol, and Stroke, accounting for Age, BMI, and Sex"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP + HighChol + Stroke + Age + BMI + Sex", data=df).fit()
df4 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df4.to_pickle('df_4.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': logit_model.llf
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

[12] ==== SURROGATE {Performer} -> "Data Analysis Code"  =============================================================================================
Here is the code to perform the analysis.
It creates the files: ['df_1.pkl', 'df_2.pkl', 'df_3.pkl', 'df_4.pkl', 'additional_results.pkl'].


# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS

## DF 1: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP", data=df).fit()
df1 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## DF 2: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighChol, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighChol", data=df).fit()
df2 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

## DF 3: "Logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against Stroke, individually"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ Stroke", data=df).fit()
df3 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df3.to_pickle('df_3.pkl')

## DF 4: "Multivariable logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, HighChol, and Stroke, accounting for Age, BMI, and Sex"
logit_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP + HighChol + Stroke + Age + BMI + Sex", data=df).fit()
df4 = pd.DataFrame(logit_model.summary2().tables[1])
df4.to_pickle('df_4.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': logit_model.llf
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS

## DF 1: "Multivariable and univariate logistic regression results and p-values for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, HighChol, and Stroke, accounting
for Age, BMI, and Sex"
# Univariate logistic regression for each variable
unadjusted_HighBP = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP", data=df).fit()
unadjusted_HighChol = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighChol", data=df).fit()
unadjusted_Stroke = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ Stroke", data=df).fit()

# Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age, BMI and Sex
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP + HighChol + Stroke + Age + BMI + Sex", data=df).fit()

# Creating a single, united DataFrame for unadjusted and adjusted logistic regression results
df1 = pd.DataFrame({
    'unadjusted_HighBP': unadjusted_HighBP.params,
    'p_value_unadjusted_HighBP': unadjusted_HighBP.pvalues,
    'adjusted_HighBP': adjusted_model.params,
    'p_value_adjusted_HighBP': adjusted_model.pvalues,
    'unadjusted_HighChol': unadjusted_HighChol.params,
    'p_value_unadjusted_HighChol': unadjusted_HighChol.pvalues,
    'adjusted_HighChol': adjusted_model.params,
    'p_value_adjusted_HighChol': adjusted_model.pvalues,
    'unadjusted_Stroke': unadjusted_Stroke.params,
    'p_value_unadjusted_Stroke': unadjusted_Stroke.pvalues,
    'adjusted_Stroke': adjusted_model.params,
    'p_value_adjusted_Stroke': adjusted_model.pvalues
})
df1 = df1.drop(['Intercept'], errors='ignore')  # Remove the intercept from the results
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS

## DF 1: "Univariate and multivariate logistic regression results for Diabetes_binary against HighBP, HighChol, and Stroke, accounting for Age, BMI,
and Sex"
# Univariate logistic regression for each variable
unadjusted_HighBP = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP", data=df).fit()
unadjusted_HighChol = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighChol", data=df).fit()
unadjusted_Stroke = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ Stroke", data=df).fit()

# Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age, BMI and Sex
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ HighBP + HighChol + Stroke + Age + BMI + Sex", data=df).fit()

# Creating a single, united DataFrame for unadjusted and adjusted logistic regression results
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Unadjusted", "Adjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
df1.loc["HighBP"] = [unadjusted_HighBP.params.HighBP, unadjusted_HighBP.pvalues.HighBP, adjusted_model.params.HighBP, adjusted_model.pvalues.HighBP]
df1.loc["HighChol"] = [unadjusted_HighChol.params.HighChol, unadjusted_HighChol.pvalues.HighChol, adjusted_model.params.HighChol,
adjusted_model.pvalues.HighChol]
df1.loc["Stroke"] = [unadjusted_Stroke.params.Stroke, unadjusted_Stroke.pvalues.Stroke, adjusted_model.params.Stroke, adjusted_model.pvalues.Stroke]

df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

Certainly, based on your feedback, let's revise the code to use a for loop to process the chosen variables systematically, create separate tables for

Here is the updated code:


# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# Prepend "Intercept" to every formula to ensure there is an intercept in the analysis
def add_intercept_to_formula(formula):
    return "Diabetes_binary ~ Intercept + " + formula.split('~')[1]

# ANALYSIS
# List of variables to be considered
variables = ["HighBP", "HighChol", "Stroke"]

## Univariate logistic regression for each variable
## DF 1: Unadjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Unadjusted"])
for var in variables:
    model_unadjusted = smf.logit(formula=add_intercept_to_formula("Diabetes_binary ~ " + var), data=df).fit()
    df1.loc[var] = [model_unadjusted.params[var], model_unadjusted.pvalues[var]]
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age, BMI, and Sex
model_vars = " + ".join(variables) + " + Age + BMI + Sex"
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula=add_intercept_to_formula("Diabetes_binary ~ " + model_vars), data=df).fit()

## DF 2: Adjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df2 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Adjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
for var in variables:
    df2.loc[var] = [adjusted_model.params[var], adjusted_model.pvalues[var]]
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}

with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# Prepend "Intercept" to every formula to ensure there is an intercept in the analysis
def add_intercept_to_formula(formula):
    return "Diabetes_binary ~ Intercept + " + formula.split('~')[1]

# ANALYSIS
# List of variables to be considered
variables = ["HighBP", "HighChol", "Stroke"]

## Univariate logistic regression for each variable
## DF 1: Unadjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Unadjusted"])
for var in variables:
    model_unadjusted = smf.logit(formula=add_intercept_to_formula("Diabetes_binary ~ " + var), data=df).fit()
    df1.loc[var] = [model_unadjusted.params[var], model_unadjusted.pvalues[var]]
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age, BMI, and Sex
model_vars = " + ".join(variables) + " + Age + BMI + Sex"
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula=add_intercept_to_formula("Diabetes_binary ~ " + model_vars), data=df).fit()

## DF 2: Adjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df2 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Adjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
for var in variables:
    df2.loc[var] = [adjusted_model.params[var], adjusted_model.pvalues[var]]
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}

with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS
# List of variables to be considered
variables = ["HighBP", "HighChol", "Stroke"]

## Univariate logistic regression for each variable
## DF 1: Unadjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Unadjusted"])
for var in variables:
    model_unadjusted = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + var, data=df).fit()
    df1.loc[var] = [model_unadjusted.params[var], model_unadjusted.pvalues[var]]
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age, BMI, and Sex
model_vars = " + ".join(variables) + " + Age + BMI + Sex"
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + model_vars, data=df).fit()

## DF 2: Adjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients and P-values
df2 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Adjusted_Coeff", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
for var in variables:
    df2.loc[var] = [adjusted_model.params[var], adjusted_model.pvalues[var]]
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}

with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

AppendMessage, Adding the debugged code as if it was the original response.
[12] ==== SURROGATE {Performer} -> "Data Analysis Code"  =============================================================================================
Here is the code to perform the analysis.
It creates the files: ['df_1.pkl', 'df_2.pkl', 'additional_results.pkl'].


# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS
# Include additional variables in the analysis
variables = ["HighBP", "HighChol", "Stroke", "BMI", "PhysActivity"]

## Univariate logistic regression for each variable
## DF 1: Unadjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients, Confidence Intervals, and P-values
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "CI_Lower_Bound", "CI_Upper_Bound", "P-Value_Unadjusted"])
for var in variables:
    model_unadjusted = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + var, data=df).fit()
    df1.loc[var] = [model_unadjusted.params[var], model_unadjusted.conf_int().loc[var][0], model_unadjusted.conf_int().loc[var][1],
model_unadjusted.pvalues[var]]
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age and Sex
model_vars = " + ".join(variables) + " + Age + Sex"
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + model_vars, data=df).fit()

## DF 2: Adjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients, Confidence Intervals, and P-values
df2 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Adjusted_Coeff", "CI_Lower_Bound", "CI_Upper_Bound", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
for var in variables:
    df2.loc[var] = [adjusted_model.params[var], adjusted_model.conf_int().loc[var][0], adjusted_model.conf_int().loc[var][1],
adjusted_model.pvalues[var]]
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}

with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# Data Analysis Code
Here is our Data Analysis Code:


# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.api as sm
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats
import pickle

# LOAD DATA
df = pd.read_csv("diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv")

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# No dataset preparations are needed.

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# No preprocessing is needed, because the existing variables are sufficient and well-defined.

# ANALYSIS
# Include additional variables in the analysis
variables = ["HighBP", "HighChol", "Stroke", "BMI", "PhysActivity"]

## Univariate logistic regression for each variable
## DF 1: Unadjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients, Confidence Intervals, and P-values
df1 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Unadjusted_Coeff", "CI_Lower_Bound", "CI_Upper_Bound", "P-Value_Unadjusted"])
for var in variables:
    model_unadjusted = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + var, data=df).fit()
    df1.loc[var] = [model_unadjusted.params[var], model_unadjusted.conf_int().loc[var][0], model_unadjusted.conf_int().loc[var][1],
model_unadjusted.pvalues[var]]
df1.to_pickle('df_1.pkl')

## Multivariate logistic regression for the variables, accounting for Age and Sex
model_vars = " + ".join(variables) + " + Age + Sex"
adjusted_model = smf.logit(formula="Diabetes_binary ~ " + model_vars, data=df).fit()

## DF 2: Adjusted Logistic Regression Coefficients, Confidence Intervals, and P-values
df2 = pd.DataFrame(columns=["Adjusted_Coeff", "CI_Lower_Bound", "CI_Upper_Bound", "P-Value_Adjusted"])
for var in variables:
    df2.loc[var] = [adjusted_model.params[var], adjusted_model.conf_int().loc[var][0], adjusted_model.conf_int().loc[var][1],
adjusted_model.pvalues[var]]
df2.to_pickle('df_2.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': len(df),
    'Goodness of fit of multivariable model': adjusted_model.llf
}

with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

\section{Code Explanation}

The provided code performs data analysis on the "diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv" dataset.

First, the necessary libraries are imported. Then, the dataset is loaded into a Pandas DataFrame.

Next, the code prepares the dataset by checking for any missing or incomplete values. However, since the original dataset has already been cleaned and
contains no missing values, no further dataset preparations are needed.

After preparing the dataset, the code proceeds to perform the analysis.

The analysis consists of two main parts: univariate logistic regression and multivariate logistic regression.

In the univariate logistic regression part, the code examines the relationship between each of the selected variables (i.e., "HighBP", "HighChol",
"Stroke", "BMI", and "PhysActivity") and the presence of diabetes. For each variable, a separate logistic regression model is fitted, and the
unadjusted logistic regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values are calculated. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df1" and
saved as a pickle file named "df_1.pkl".

In the multivariate logistic regression part, the code builds a logistic regression model that includes all the selected variables (i.e., "HighBP",
"HighChol", "Stroke", "BMI", and "PhysActivity"), as well as covariates related to age and sex. The model is fitted, and the adjusted logistic
regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values are calculated. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df2" and saved as a pickle file
named "df_2.pkl".

Additionally, the code saves some additional results in a separate pickle file named "additional_results.pkl". These additional results include the
total number of observations in the dataset and the goodness of fit of the multivariable logistic regression model.

In summary, the code performs both univariate and multivariate logistic regression analyses to explore the relationship between selected variables and
the presence of diabetes. The results are saved as pickle files for further analysis and reporting.

```
\section{Code Explanation}

The provided code performs data analysis on the "diabetes\_binary\_health\_indicators\_BRFSS2015.csv" dataset.

The code first imports necessary libraries and loads the dataset into a Pandas DataFrame.

Next, the dataset is prepared by checking for missing or incomplete values. However, since the original dataset has already been cleaned and contains
no missing values, no further dataset preparations are needed.

The code then proceeds to perform the analysis, which involves two main parts: univariate logistic regression and multivariate logistic regression.

In the univariate logistic regression part, the code examines the relationship between each of the selected variables (i.e., "HighBP", "HighChol",
"Stroke", "BMI", and "PhysActivity") and the presence of diabetes. For each variable, a separate logistic regression model is fitted. The code
calculates the unadjusted logistic regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df1" and
saved as a pickle file named "df\_1.pkl".

In the multivariate logistic regression part, the code builds a logistic regression model that includes all the selected variables, as well as
covariates related to age and sex. The model is fitted, and the adjusted logistic regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values are
calculated. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df2" and saved as a pickle file named "df\_2.pkl".

Additionally, the code saves some additional results, such as the total number of observations in the dataset and the goodness of fit of the
multivariable logistic regression model. These additional results are stored in a separate pickle file named "additional\_results.pkl".

In summary, the provided code conducts univariate and multivariate logistic regression analyses to explore the relationship between the selected
variables and the presence of diabetes. The results are saved as pickle files for further analysis and reporting.
````

\section{Code Explanation}

The provided code performs data analysis on the "diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv" dataset.

First, the necessary libraries are imported. Then, the dataset is loaded into a Pandas DataFrame.

Next, the code prepares the dataset by checking for any missing or incomplete values. However, since the original dataset has already been cleaned and
contains no missing values, no further dataset preparations are needed.

After preparing the dataset, the code proceeds to perform the analysis.

The analysis consists of two main parts: univariate logistic regression and multivariate logistic regression.

In the univariate logistic regression part, the code examines the relationship between each of the selected variables (i.e., "HighBP", "HighChol",
"Stroke", "BMI", and "PhysActivity") and the presence of diabetes. For each variable, a separate logistic regression model is fitted, and the
unadjusted logistic regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values are calculated. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df1" and
saved as a pickle file named "df_1.pkl".

In the multivariate logistic regression part, the code builds a logistic regression model that includes all the selected variables (i.e., "HighBP",
"HighChol", "Stroke", "BMI", and "PhysActivity"), as well as covariates related to age and sex. The model is fitted, and the adjusted logistic
regression coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values are calculated. These results are stored in the DataFrame "df2" and saved as a pickle file
named "df_2.pkl".

Additionally, the code saves some additional results in a separate pickle file named "additional_results.pkl". These additional results include the
total number of observations in the dataset and the goodness of fit of the multivariable logistic regression model.

In summary, the code performs both univariate and multivariate logistic regression analyses to explore the relationship between selected variables and
the presence of diabetes. The results are saved as pickle files for further analysis and reporting.

```latex
\section{Code Explanation}

The presented Python script executes a statistical examination of a dataset consisting of various health indicators related to diabetes. At the core
of the analysis are two regression methodologies employed to discern the relationship between diabetes and selected predictor variables.

Primarily, univariate logistic regression models are individually constructed for a subset of predictors: high blood pressure (HighBP), high
cholesterol (HighChol), stroke, Body Mass Index (BMI), and physical activity (PhysActivity). Each model quantifies the effect of one predictor on the
likelihood of having diabetes, yielding unadjusted coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values. The outcomes of these univariate analyses are
synthesized into a Pandas DataFrame (df1) for subsequent documentation.

Subsequently, multivariate logistic regression is applied to integrate the influence of all selected predictors while adjusting for age and gender.
This comprehensive approach allows for the assessment of each variable's contribution to the probability of diabetes occurrence while accounting for
possible confounding effects of the controlled covariates. Adjusted coefficients, confidence intervals, and p-values from this analysis are aggregated
into another DataFrame (df2).

In the culmination of the process, auxiliary results comprising the model's goodness-of-fit statistic (likelihood function value) and the total sample
size are preserved. These are serialized into a pickle file named "additional_results.pkl," which encapsulates the overall fit quality of the
multivariate model and contextual details of the dataset under study.
```

Please write a Python code to convert and re-style the "df_?.pkl" dataframes created by our "Data Analysis Code" into latex tables/figures suitable
for our scientific paper.

Your code should use the following 4 custom functions provided for import from `my_utils`:


def to_latex_with_note(df, filename: str, caption: str, label: str,
                       note: str = None, glossary: Dict[str, str] = None, **kwargs):
    """
    Saves a DataFrame as a LaTeX table with optional note and glossary added below the table.

    Parameters:
    - df, filename, caption, label: as in `df.to_latex`.
    - note (optional): Additional note below the table.
    - glossary (optional): Dictionary mapping abbreviations to full names.
    - **kwargs: Additional arguments for `df.to_latex`.
    """

def to_figure_with_note(df, filename: str, caption: str, label: str,
                        note: str = None, glossary: Dict[str, str] = None,
                        x: Optional[str] = None, y: Optional[str] = None, kind: str = 'line',
                        use_index: bool = True,
                        xlabel: str = None, ylabel: str = None,
                        logx: bool = False, logy: bool = False,
                        xerr: str = None, yerr: str = None,
                        x_ci: Union[str, Tuple[str, str]] = None, y_ci: Union[str, Tuple[str, str]] = None,
                        x_p_value: str = None, y_p_value: str = None,
                        ):
    """
    Saves a DataFrame to a LaTeX figure with caption and optional glossary added below the figure.

    Parameters:
    `df`: DataFrame to plot (with column names and index as scientific labels).
    `filename` (str): name of a .tex file to create (a matching .png file will also be created).
    `caption` (str): Caption for the figure (can be multi-line).
    `label` (str): Latex label for the figure, 'figure:xxx'.
    `glossary` (optional, dict): Dictionary mapping abbreviated df col/row labels to full names.

    Parameters for df.plot():
    `x` / `y` (optional, str): Column name for x-axis / y-axis values.
    `kind` (str): Type of plot: 'line', 'scatter', 'bar'.
    `use_index` (bool): If True, use the index as x-axis values.
    `logx` / `logy` (bool): If True, use log scale for x/y axis.
    `xerr` / `yerr` (optional, str): Column name for x/y error bars.
    `xlabel` / `ylabel` (optional, str): Label for x/y axis.

    Additional plotting options:
    `x_p_value` / `y_p_value` (optional, str): Column name for x/y p-values to show as stars above data points.
        p-values are converted to: '***' if < 0.001, '**' if < 0.01, '*' if < 0.05, 'NS' if >= 0.05.

    Instead of xerr/yerr, you can directly provide confidence intervals:
    `x_ci` / `y_ci` (optional, str or (str, str)): an be either a single column name where each row contains
        a 2-element tuple (n x 2 matrix when expanded), or a list containing two column names
        representing the lower and upper bounds of the confidence interval.

    Note on error bars (explanation for y-axis is provided, x-axis is analogous):
    Either `yerr` or `y_ci` can be provided, but not both.
    If `yerr` is provided, the plotted error bars are (df[y]-df[yerr], df[y]+df[yerr]).
    If `y_ci` is provided, the plotted error bars are (df[y_ci][0], df[y_ci][1]).
    Note that unlike yerr, the y_ci are NOT added to the nominal df[y] values.
    Instead, the provided y_ci values should flank the nominal df[y] values.
    """

def is_str_in_df(df: pd.DataFrame, s: str):
    return any(s in level for level in getattr(df.index, 'levels', [df.index]) + getattr(df.columns, 'levels', [df.columns]))

AbbrToNameDef = Dict[Any, Tuple[Optional[str], Optional[str]]]

def split_mapping(abbrs_to_names_and_definitions: AbbrToNameDef):
    abbrs_to_names = {abbr: name for abbr, (name, definition) in abbrs_to_names_and_definitions.items() if name is not None}
    names_to_definitions = {name or abbr: definition for abbr, (name, definition) in abbrs_to_names_and_definitions.items() if definition is not None}
    return abbrs_to_names, names_to_definitions

- Remains abbreviated, or not self-explanatory, even after renaming.
- Is an ordinal/categorical variable that requires clarification of the meaning of each of its possible values.
- Contains unclear notation, like '*' or ':'
- Represents a numeric variable that has units, that need to be specified.

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
from my_utils import to_latex_with_note, to_figure_with_note, is_str_in_df, split_mapping, AbbrToNameDef

# PREPARATION FOR ALL TABLES AND FIGURES
shared_mapping: AbbrToNameDef = {
    "HighBP": ("High Blood Pressure", "High Blood Pressure (0=no, 1=yes)"),
    "HighChol": ("High Cholesterol", " High Cholesterol (0=no, 1=yes)"),
    "Stroke": ("Stroke", "Stroke (0=no, 1=yes)"),
    "BMI": ("Body Mass Index", "Body Mass Index"),
    "PhysActivity": ("Physical Activity", "Physical Activity in past 30 days (0=no, 1=yes)")
}

# DF 1
df1 = pd.read_pickle('df_1.pkl')

# Format values:
# Not Applicable

mapping1 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df1, k))
mapping1 |= {
    'Unadjusted_Coeff': ('Unadjusted Coefficient', None),
    'CI_Lower_Bound':   ('CI Lower Bound', '95% Confidence Interval'),

# Overall Description of the Dataset
## General Description
The dataset includes diabetes related factors extracted from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS), year 2015.
The original BRFSS, from which this dataset is derived, is a health-related telephone survey that is collected annually by the CDC.
Each year, the survey collects responses from over 400,000 Americans on health-related risk behaviors, chronic health conditions, and the use of
preventative services. These features are either questions directly asked of participants, or calculated variables based on individual participant
responses.

```latex
\title{The Impact of Lifestyle and Health Indicators on Diabetes Prevalence}

\begin{abstract}
The pervasive nature of diabetes poses a global health challenge, with implications reaching far beyond individual health to societal and economic
burdens. Addressing this issue requires identifying and understanding the influence of various risk factors on the development of the disease. This
study aims to elucidate the relationship between lifestyle measures and diabetes incidence, a question of considerable public health significance.
Utilizing a comprehensive dataset from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System, this study investigates over a quarter-million Americans,
focusing on health-related behaviors and conditions as they relate to diabetes. Our methodological approach involves univariate and multivariate
logistic regression analyses to discern the independent and combined effects of several key variables including blood pressure, cholesterol levels,
history of stroke, body mass index, and physical activity, while adjusting for age and sex. The results affirm the strong association of lifestyle
factors with diabetes prevalence, demonstrating that both singular and cumulative health indicators significantly relate to diabetes risk. Notably,
physical activity emerges as a protective factor, whereas the remaining variables exhibit a positive correlation with the condition. Despite its
reliance on self-reported data, which may introduce response biases, this study's findings underpin critical lifestyle determinants associated with
diabetes and support targeted intervention strategies, affirming the significance of prevention and lifestyle modification.
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{The Impact of Lifestyle and Health Indicators on Diabetes Prevalence}

\begin{abstract}
The pervasive nature of diabetes poses a global health challenge, with implications reaching far beyond individual health to societal and economic
burdens. Addressing this issue requires identifying and understanding the influence of various risk factors on the development of the disease. This
study aims to elucidate the relationship between lifestyle measures and diabetes incidence, a question of considerable public health significance.
Utilizing a comprehensive dataset from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System, this study investigates over a quarter-million Americans,
focusing on health-related behaviors and conditions as they relate to diabetes. Our methodological approach involves univariate and multivariate
logistic regression analyses to discern the independent and combined effects of several key variables including blood pressure, cholesterol levels,
history of stroke, body mass index, and physical activity, while adjusting for age and sex. The results affirm the strong association of lifestyle
factors with diabetes prevalence, demonstrating that both singular and cumulative health indicators significantly relate to diabetes risk. Notably,
physical activity emerges as a protective factor, whereas the remaining variables exhibit a positive correlation with the condition. Despite its
reliance on self-reported data, which may introduce response biases, this study's findings underpin critical lifestyle determinants associated with
diabetes and support targeted intervention strategies, affirming the significance of prevention and lifestyle modification.
\end{abstract}
```

# Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)
## General Description
The dataset includes diabetes related factors extracted from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS), year
\hypertarget{S0a}{2015}.
The original BRFSS, from which this dataset is derived, is a health-related telephone survey that is collected annually by the CDC.
Each year, the survey collects responses from over \hypertarget{S1a}{400,000} Americans on health-related risk behaviors, chronic health conditions,
and the use of preventative services. These features are either questions directly asked of participants, or calculated variables based on individual
participant responses.

## Data Files
The dataset consists of 1 data file:

### "diabetes_binary_health_indicators_BRFSS2015.csv"
The csv file is a clean dataset of \hypertarget{T0a}{253,680} responses (rows) and \hypertarget{T0b}{22} features (columns).
All rows with missing values were removed from the original dataset; the current file contains no missing values.

The columns in the dataset are:

#1 `Diabetes_binary`: (int, bool) Diabetes (\hypertarget{T1a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T1b}{1}=yes)
#2 `HighBP`: (int, bool) High Blood Pressure (\hypertarget{T2a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T2b}{1}=yes)
#3 `HighChol`: (int, bool) High Cholesterol (\hypertarget{T3a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T3b}{1}=yes)
#4 `CholCheck`: (int, bool) Cholesterol check in \hypertarget{T4a}{5} years (\hypertarget{T4b}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T4c}{1}=yes)
#5 `BMI`: (int, numerical) Body Mass Index
#6 `Smoker`: (int, bool) (\hypertarget{T5a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T5b}{1}=yes)
#7 `Stroke`: (int, bool) Stroke (\hypertarget{T6a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T6b}{1}=yes)
#8 `HeartDiseaseorAttack`: (int, bool) coronary heart disease (CHD) or myocardial infarction (MI), (\hypertarget{T7a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T7b}{1}=yes)
#9 `PhysActivity`: (int, bool) Physical Activity in past \hypertarget{T8a}{30} days (\hypertarget{T8b}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T8c}{1}=yes)
#10 `Fruits`: (int, bool) Consume one fruit or more each day (\hypertarget{T9a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T9b}{1}=yes)
#11 `Veggies`: (int, bool) Consume one Vegetable or more each day (\hypertarget{T10a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T10b}{1}=yes)
#12 `HvyAlcoholConsump` (int, bool) Heavy drinkers (\hypertarget{T11a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T11b}{1}=yes)
#13 `AnyHealthcare` (int, bool) Have any kind of health care coverage (\hypertarget{T12a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T12b}{1}=yes)
#14 `NoDocbcCost` (int, bool) Was there a time in the past \hypertarget{T13a}{12} months when you needed to see a doctor but could not because of
cost? (\hypertarget{T13b}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T13c}{1}=yes)
#15 `GenHlth` (int, ordinal) self-reported health (\hypertarget{T14a}{1}=excellent, \hypertarget{T14b}{2}=very good, \hypertarget{T14c}{3}=good,
\hypertarget{T14d}{4}=fair, \hypertarget{T14e}{5}=poor)
#16 `MentHlth` (int, ordinal) How many days during the past \hypertarget{T15a}{30} days was your mental health not good? (\hypertarget{T15b}{1} -
\hypertarget{T15c}{30} days)
#17 `PhysHlth` (int, ordinal) Hor how many days during the past \hypertarget{T16a}{30} days was your physical health not good? (\hypertarget{T16b}{1}
- \hypertarget{T16c}{30} days)
#18 `DiffWalk` (int, bool) Do you have serious difficulty walking or climbing stairs? (\hypertarget{T17a}{0}=no, \hypertarget{T17b}{1}=yes)
#19 `Sex` (int, categorical) Sex (\hypertarget{T18a}{0}=female, \hypertarget{T18b}{1}=male)
#20 `Age` (int, ordinal) Age, \hypertarget{T19a}{13}-level age category in intervals of \hypertarget{T19b}{5} years (\hypertarget{T19c}{1}=
\hypertarget{T19d}{18} - \hypertarget{T19e}{24}, \hypertarget{T19f}{2}= \hypertarget{T19g}{25} - \hypertarget{T19h}{29}, ..., \hypertarget{T19i}{12}=
\hypertarget{T19j}{75} - \hypertarget{T19k}{79}, \hypertarget{T19l}{13} = \hypertarget{T19m}{80} or older)
#21 `Education` (int, ordinal) Education level on a scale of \hypertarget{T20a}{1} - \hypertarget{T20b}{6} (\hypertarget{T20c}{1}=Never attended
school, \hypertarget{T20d}{2}=Elementary, \hypertarget{T20e}{3}=Some high school, \hypertarget{T20f}{4}=High school, \hypertarget{T20g}{5}=Some
college, \hypertarget{T20h}{6}=College)
#22 `Income` (int, ordinal) Income scale on a scale of \hypertarget{T21a}{1} to \hypertarget{T21b}{8} (\hypertarget{T21c}{1}=
<=\hypertarget{T21d}{10}K, \hypertarget{T21e}{2}= <=\hypertarget{T21f}{15}K, \hypertarget{T21g}{3}= <=\hypertarget{T21h}{20}K, \hypertarget{T21i}{4}=

<=\hypertarget{T21p}{75}K, \hypertarget{T21q}{8}= >75K)

Based on the material provided above ("Title and Abstract", "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)", "Data Analysis Code",
"Displayitems of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets"), please write only the `Results`
section for a Nature Communications article.
Do not write any other parts!

Use the following guidelines when writing the Results:

* Include 3-4 paragraphs, each focusing on one of the displayitems:
You should typically have a separate paragraph describing each of the Tables/Figures. In each such paragraph, indicate the motivation/question for the
analysis, the methodology, and only then describe the results. You should describe what we see and learn from each Table/Figure. You should refer to
the Tables/Figures by their labels (using \ref{table:xxx}, \ref{figure:xxx}), but do not add the displayitems themselves (they will be added later
automatically).

* Story-like flow:
It is often nice to have a story-like flow between the paragraphs, so that the reader can follow the analysis process with emphasis on the
reasoning/motivation behind each analysis step.
For example, the first sentence of each paragraph can be a story-guiding sentences like:
"First, to understand whether xxx, we conducted a simple analysis of ..."; "Then, to test yyy, we performed a ..."; "Finally, to further verify the
effect of zzz, we tested whether ...".

* Conclude with a summary of the results:
You can summarize the results at the end, with a sentence like: "In summary, these results show ...", or "Taken together, these results suggest ...".
IMPORTANT NOTE: Your summary SHOULD NOT include a discussion of conclusions, implications, limitations, or of future work. (These will be added later
as part the Discussion section, not the Results section).

* Numeric values:

- Sources:
You can extract numeric values from the above provided sources: "Displayitems of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results
(additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets", and "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)".
All numeric values in these sources have a \hypertarget with a unique label.

- Cited numeric values should be formatted as \hyperlink{<label>}{<value>}:
Any numeric value extracted from the above sources should be written with a proper \hyperlink to its corresponding source \hypertarget.

- Dependent values should be calculated using the \num command.
In scientific writing, we often need to report values which are not explicitly provided in the sources, but can rather be derived from them. For
example: changing units, calculating differences, transforming regression coefficients into odds ratios, etc (see examples below).

To derive such dependent values, please use the \num{<formula>, "explanation"} command.
The <formula> contains a calculation, which will be automatically replaced with its result upon pdf compilation.
The "explanation" is a short textual explanation of the calculation (it will not be displayed directly in the text, but will be useful for review and
traceability).

- Toy example for citing and calculating numeric values:

Suppose our provided source data includes:

No-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z1a}{0.65}
With-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z2a}{0.87}

Treatment regression:
coef = \hypertarget{Z3a}{0.17}, STD = \hypertarget{Z3b}{0.072}, pvalue = <\hypertarget{Z3c}{1e-6}


Then, here are some examples of proper ways to report these provided source values:

The no-treatment control group had a response of \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} while the with-treatment group had a response of \hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87}.

The regression coefficient for the treatment was \hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} with a standard deviation of \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072} (P-value: <
\hyperlink{Z3c}{1e-6}).


And here are some examples of proper ways to calculate dependent values, using the \num command:

The difference in response was \num{\hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87} - \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65}, "Difference between responses with and without treatment"}.

The treatment odds ratio was \num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17}), "Translating the treatment regression coefficient to odds ratio"} (CI:
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} - 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "low CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"},
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} + 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "high CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"}).


* Accuracy:
Make sure that you are only mentioning details that are explicitly found within the Tables, Figures and Numerical Values.

* Unknown values:
If we need to include a numeric value that is not explicitly given in the Tables/Figures or "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with
hypertargets", and cannot be derived from them using the \num command, then indicate `[unknown]` instead of the numeric value.

For example:

The no-treatment response was \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} (STD: [unknown]).



Write in tex format, escaping any math or symbols that needs tex escapes.

```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand the univariate relation between lifestyle factors and diabetes prevalence, we conducted simple logistic regressions for each
considered variable independently. The analysis was based on a sizeable cohort of \hyperlink{R0a}{253,680} participants. Figure
\ref{figure:Unadjusted_Coefficients} represents the unadjusted logistic regression coefficients indicating the strength of association between
lifestyle variables and the odds of having diabetes. High blood pressure was associated with an increased likelihood of diabetes, with a coefficient
of \hyperlink{A0a}{1.617} and a significance below \hyperlink{A0c}{0.0001}. High cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index (BMI) were similarly
associated with higher odds of diabetes, with coefficients of \hyperlink{A1a}{1.18}, \hyperlink{A2a}{1.12}, and \num{\hyperlink{A3a}{0.0786},
"Coefficient for BMI"} respectively, each also showing high statistical significance (P-value: < \hyperlink{A3c}{0.0001}). In contrast, physical
activity was shown to be a protective factor, with an inverse association coefficient of \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.7134} and a similarly high level of
significance.

Then, to adjust for potential confounders, a multivariate logistic regression analysis incorporating age and sex was performed. Figure
\ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} illustrates the adjusted logistic regression coefficients, presenting a comprehensive view of how the independent
variables relate to diabetes risk when accounting for age and sex. Notably, after adjustment, the strength of the associations diminished slightly,
but remained significant. High blood pressure (\hyperlink{B0a}{0.9799}), high cholesterol (\hyperlink{B1a}{0.6891}), stroke (\hyperlink{B2a}{0.5472}),
and BMI (\num{\hyperlink{B3a}{0.07242}, "Coefficient for BMI adjusted"}) continued to exhibit a positive association with the presence of diabetes.
Physical activity remained the only variable inversely associated with diabetes prevalence with an adjusted coefficient of \hyperlink{B4a}{-0.351}.
All adjusted associations maintained a P-value less than \hyperlink{B4c}{0.0001}, underscoring the robustness of these lifestyle variables in relation
to diabetes risk.

Finally, the goodness of fit for the multivariable logistic regression model was assessed to verify the model's explanatory power regarding the
variability in diabetes occurrence. The model's log-likelihood function, a measure of goodness of fit, was given a value of \hyperlink{R1a}{-85980},
indicating a reasonable fit of the model in capturing the relationship between the predictors and diabetes status across the surveyed population.

In summary, the results from both unadjusted and adjusted analyses consistently suggest that lifestyle factors such as high blood pressure, high
cholesterol, stroke, and higher BMI significantly increase the likelihood of diabetes, while engagement in physical activity acts as a protective
factor against this disease.
```

```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand the univariate relation between lifestyle factors and diabetes prevalence, we conducted simple logistic regressions for each
considered variable independently. The analysis was based on a sizeable cohort of \hyperlink{R0a}{253,680} participants. Figure
\ref{figure:Unadjusted_Coefficients} represents the unadjusted logistic regression coefficients indicating the strength of association between
lifestyle variables and the odds of having diabetes. High blood pressure was associated with an increased likelihood of diabetes, with a coefficient
of \hyperlink{A0a}{1.617} and a significant P-value (P $<$ \hyperlink{A0c}{0.0001}). High cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index (BMI) were
similarly associated with higher odds of diabetes, with coefficients of \hyperlink{A1a}{1.18}, \hyperlink{A2a}{1.12}, and \hyperlink{A3a}{0.0786}
respectively, and each showed a high significance level (P $<$ \hyperlink{A3c}{0.0001}). In contrast, physical activity was shown to be a protective
factor, with an inverse association coefficient of \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.7134} and a P-value of less than \hyperlink{A4c}{0.0001}.

Then, to adjust for potential confounders, a multivariate logistic regression analysis incorporating age and sex was performed. Figure
\ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} illustrates the adjusted logistic regression coefficients, presenting a comprehensive view of how the independent
variables relate to diabetes risk when accounting for age and sex. Notably, after adjustment, the strength of associations diminished slightly but
remained significant. High blood pressure (\hyperlink{B0a}{0.9799}), high cholesterol (\hyperlink{B1a}{0.6891}), stroke (\hyperlink{B2a}{0.5472}), and
BMI (\hyperlink{B3a}{0.07242}) continued to exhibit a positive association with the presence of diabetes. Physical activity remained as the only
variable inversely associated with diabetes prevalence with an adjusted coefficient of \hyperlink{B4a}{-0.351}. All adjusted associations maintained
significance with a P-value less than \hyperlink{B4c}{0.0001}.

Finally, to assess the goodness of fit for the multivariable logistic regression model, we examined the model's log-likelihood value. The log-
likelihood function, a measure of the goodness of fit, was \hyperlink{R1a}{-8.598e+04}, indicating a reasonable fit to explain the variability in
diabetes occurrence across the surveyed population.

In summary, the results from both unadjusted and adjusted analyses consistently suggest that lifestyle factors such as high blood pressure, high
cholesterol, stroke, and higher BMI significantly increase the likelihood of diabetes, while engagement in physical activity acts as a protective
factor against this disease.
```

Using GPT4_TURBO.
[16] ==== ASSISTANT{'model_engine': <ModelEngine.GPT4_TURBO: 'gpt-4-1106-preview'>} {Performer} -> "Writing: Results"  ===============================
```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand whether individual health indicators exhibit a direct association with diabetes, univariate logistic regression analyses were
conducted for each risk factor. Within a large sample of \hyperlink{R0a}{253,680} participants, univariate logistic regression revealed that each
investigated variable was statistically significant (P $<$ 0.0001). High blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index were positively
associated with the likelihood of diabetes with coefficients of \hyperlink{A0a}{1.617}, \hyperlink{A1a}{1.18}, \hyperlink{A2a}{1.12}, and
\num{\hyperlink{A3a}{0.0786}, "coefficient value for BMI"} respectively as demonstrated in Figure \ref{figure:Unadjusted_Coefficients}. Physical
activity presented a negative association, implying a protective effect against diabetes development with a coefficient of \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.7134}.

Then, to examine the influence of these factors while controlling for confounding variables, we performed multivariate logistic regression accounting
for age and sex. After adjustment, Figure \ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} indicates that the variables maintained their directional influence on
diabetes prevalence with updated coefficients: High blood pressure (\hyperlink{B0a}{0.9799}), high cholesterol (\hyperlink{B1a}{0.6891}), stroke
(\hyperlink{B2a}{0.5472}), and BMI (\hyperlink{B3a}{0.07242}) each remained positively associated with diabetes; physical activity
(\hyperlink{B4a}{-0.351}) continued demonstrating a protective relationship. All findings were statistically significant (P $<$ 0.0001).

Finally, the goodness of fit for the multivariate model was assessed by the log-likelihood statistic, which yielded a value of
\hyperlink{R1a}{-8.598e+04}, suggesting an adequate fit of the proposed model for the relationship between lifestyle indicators and diabetes within
the analyzed population.

In summary, the results indicate that high blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, higher BMI are associated with increased diabetes risk, while
physical activity is inversely associated, even after controlling for age and sex. The prevalence of diabetes in the study's cohort underlines the
significance of lifestyle and health indicators in understanding the disease.
```

Using GPT4_TURBO.
[16] ==== ASSISTANT{'model_engine': <ModelEngine.GPT4_TURBO: 'gpt-4-1106-preview'>} {Performer} -> "Writing: Results"  ===============================
```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand whether individual health indicators exhibit a direct association with diabetes, univariate logistic regression analyses were
conducted for each risk factor. Within a large sample of \hyperlink{R0a}{253,680} participants, univariate logistic regression revealed that each
investigated variable was statistically significant. High blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index were positively associated
with the likelihood of diabetes with coefficients of \hyperlink{A0a}{1.617}, \hyperlink{A1a}{1.18}, \hyperlink{A2a}{1.12}, and \hyperlink{A3a}{0.0786}
respectively as demonstrated in Figure \ref{figure:Unadjusted_Coefficients}. Physical activity presented a negative association, implying a protective
effect against diabetes development with a coefficient of \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.7134}.

Then, to examine the influence of these factors while controlling for confounding variables, we performed multivariate logistic regression accounting
for age and sex. After adjustment, Figure \ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} indicates that the variables maintained their directional influence on
diabetes prevalence with updated coefficients: High blood pressure (\hyperlink{B0a}{0.9799}), high cholesterol (\hyperlink{B1a}{0.6891}), stroke
(\hyperlink{B2a}{0.5472}), and BMI (\hyperlink{B3a}{0.07242}) each remained positively associated with diabetes; physical activity
(\hyperlink{B4a}{-0.351}) continued demonstrating a protective relationship. All findings were statistically significant.

Finally, the goodness of fit for the multivariate model was assessed by the log-likelihood statistic, which yielded a value of
\hyperlink{R1a}{-8.598e+04}, suggesting an adequate fit of the proposed model for the relationship between lifestyle indicators and diabetes within
the analyzed population.

In summary, the results indicate that high blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and higher BMI are associated with increased diabetes risk, while
physical activity is inversely associated, even after controlling for age and sex. The prevalence of diabetes in the study's cohort underlines the
significance of lifestyle and health indicators in understanding the disease.
```

```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand whether individual health indicators exhibit a direct association with diabetes, univariate logistic regression analyses were
conducted for each risk factor. Within a large sample of 253,680 participants, univariate logistic regression revealed that each investigated variable
was statistically significant. High blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index were positively associated with the likelihood of

for age and sex. After adjustment, Figure \ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} indicates that the variables maintained their directional influence on

remained positively associated with diabetes; physical activity (-0.351) continued demonstrating a protective relationship. All findings were
statistically significant.

an adequate fit of the proposed model for the relationship between lifestyle indicators and diabetes within the analyzed population.

In summary, the results indicate that high blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and higher BMI are associated with increased diabetes risk, while
physical activity is inversely associated, even after controlling for age and sex. The prevalence of diabetes in the study's cohort underlines the
significance of lifestyle and health indicators in understanding the disease.
```

- Ensure that numerical values mentioned match those shown in the provided figures; confirm the coefficients, as there might be a formatting issue
such as missing decimals or improper rounding off.
- When discussing statistical significance, avoid vagueness such as "statistically significant" without providing specific P-values or significance
levels, particularly when these details are available in the figures being referenced.
- Consider including a short sentence or clause to clarify what the reported coefficients represent (e.g., odds ratio), as this helps in the
interpretation of the results for readers not familiar with logistic regression output.
- The abstract mentions adjusting for additional variables beyond age and sex. If these were indeed included in the adjusted model, the corresponding
details need to be acknowledged in the Results section as well.
- Since the coefficients after adjustment have changed, consider mentioning or discussing the possible reasons for this change to guide the reader on
the potential implications of confounders on the risk estimates.
- Reference the exact figure labels as per the earlier part of the paper (if they are "Figure 1," "Figure 2," etc., these should be consistent
throughout).
- Clarify the goodness of fit description; log-likelihood is a less-intuitive measure for those unfamiliar with statistical modeling. A brief
explanation could be added to aid the reader's understanding.
- The summary briefly recapitulates findings but could also mention the scale of effect or the relative strength of associations to emphasize the most
impactful factors.

[7] ----- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Title and Abstract"  <background_paper_sections:results>  -------------------------------------------------------
# Results Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand whether individual health indicators exhibit a direct association with diabetes, univariate logistic regression analyses were
conducted for each risk factor. Within a large sample of 253,680 participants, univariate logistic regression revealed that each investigated variable
was statistically significant. High blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and body mass index were positively associated with the likelihood of
diabetes with coefficients of 1.617, 1.18, 1.12, and 0.0786 respectively as demonstrated in Figure \ref{figure:Unadjusted_Coefficients}. Physical
activity presented a negative association, implying a protective effect against diabetes development with a coefficient of -0.7134.

Then, to examine the influence of these factors while controlling for confounding variables, we performed multivariate logistic regression accounting
for age and sex. After adjustment, Figure \ref{figure:Adjusted_Coefficients} indicates that the variables maintained their directional influence on
diabetes prevalence with updated coefficients: High blood pressure (0.9799), high cholesterol (0.6891), stroke (0.5472), and BMI (0.07242) each
remained positively associated with diabetes; physical activity (-0.351) continued demonstrating a protective relationship. All findings were
statistically significant.

Finally, the goodness of fit for the multivariate model was assessed by the log-likelihood statistic, which yielded a value of -8.598e+04, suggesting
an adequate fit of the proposed model for the relationship between lifestyle indicators and diabetes within the analyzed population.

In summary, the results indicate that high blood pressure, high cholesterol, stroke, and higher BMI are associated with increased diabetes risk, while
physical activity is inversely associated, even after controlling for age and sex. The prevalence of diabetes in the study's cohort underlines the
significance of lifestyle and health indicators in understanding the disease.
```

The Title should:
* be short and meaningful.
* convey the main message, focusing on discovery not on methodology nor on the data source.
* not include punctuation marks, such as ":,;" characters.

The Abstract should provide a concise, interesting to read, single-paragraph summary of the paper, with the following structure:
* short statement of the subject and its importance.
* description of the research gap/question/motivation.
* short, non-technical, description of the dataset used and a non-technical explanation of the methodology.
* summary of each of the main results. Summarize each key result which is evident from the displayitems, but without referring to specific numeric
values from the displayitems.
* statement of limitations and implications.


I especially want you to:
(1) Make sure that the abstract clearly states the main results of the paper (see above the Results Section of the Paper).
(2) Make sure that the abstract correctly defines the literature gap/question/motivation (see above Literature Searches for list of related papers).

```latex
\title{Lifestyle Determinants of Diabetes: A Quantitative Assessment of Health Risk Factors}

\begin{abstract}
Diabetes represents a critical public health predicament, with escalating prevalence and far-reaching implications for society and healthcare systems.
This study addresses the critical research gap of quantifying the impact of modifiable lifestyle and health predictors on diabetes risk. We leverage a
substantial cohort from the CDC'S Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System dataset, encompassing diverse health-related behaviors and conditions.
Utilizing robust statistical methods, the study conducts a comprehensive investigation into the associations between critical health indicators such
as blood pressure, cholesterol, stroke history, body mass index, and physical activity with the occurrence of diabetes. Our results underscore the
highly significant association of elevated blood pressure, cholesterol, stroke history, and higher BMI with increased diabetes risk, whereas physical
activity exhibits a protective influence against diabetes prevalence. These findings remain robust even after adjusting for age and sex, indicating
the intrinsic ties between lifestyle factors and diabetes. Despite inherent limitations of self-reported data, the study substantiates the pivotal
role of modifiable lifestyle factors in diabetes management and underscores the urgency for public health interventions targeting these variables.
Importantly, our results contribute to a more nuanced understanding of the diabetes epidemic and lay the groundwork for tailored preventive
strategies.
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Diabetes Risk Amplified by Lifestyle and Health Metrics}

\beginformat{abstract}
The escalating global incidence of diabetes underscores its status as a prime public health challenge with substantive implications for societal
health dynamics and economic stability. Addressing this escalating health concern, our study seeks to fill the research void by delineating how
lifestyle factors and health indicators interplay to modify the risk of diabetes. Drawing from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System,
we analyzed a dataset representative of over 400,000 Americans to investigate health-related behaviors and their linkage to diabetes incidence.
Through a nuanced approach employing both univariate and multivariate logistic regression analyses, we evaluated the relationship of blood pressure,
cholesterol levels, stroke history, body mass index, and physical activity with diabetes risk, whilst accounting for demographic variables. The
research unveils that elevated blood pressure, higher cholesterol, prior incidence of stroke, and increased BMI significantly heighten diabetes risk,
whereas heightened physical activity confers a protective effect. These relations persist even after control for age and sex differences, emphasizing
the integral role of these factors in the diabetes landscape. Although the study acknowledges the limitations inherent in self-reported data, the
implications of this research are vast, providing concrete evidence in support of health policy that promotes lifestyle modifications as a cornerstone
for diabetes prevention.
\end{abstract}
```

# Title and Abstract
```latex
\title{Diabetes Risk and Lifestyle Correlates Quantified through National Health Data}

\begin{abstract}
The current diabetes epidemic not only compromises individual health but also burdens healthcare systems worldwide. Addressing this condition
necessitates the elucidation of modifiable risk factors to inform effective intervention strategies. This study bridges the knowledge gap by
quantifying the impact of lifestyle factors on diabetes prevalence. Capitalizing on data from the CDC's Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System,
our analysis encompasses a cohort of more than 250,000 Americans, focusing on key health-related behaviors and conditions. The method involves
univariate and multivariate logistic regression analyses to unpack the individual and aggregate contributions of blood pressure, cholesterol, stroke
history, body mass index, and physical activity to diabetes susceptibility. Results reveal significant and differential impacts of health indicators
on diabetes risks, with physical activity conferring a protective effect. The robustness of these associations persists after demographic adjustments,
cementing the direct relevance of these lifestyle factors to diabetes. Self-reporting in data collection is an acknowledged limitation that might bias
the findings. Nevertheless, our results highlight actionable health domains and reinforce the imperative for targeted public health initiatives. The
findings advance our understanding of diabetes pathogenesis and support the prioritization of lifestyle modifications in prevention efforts.
\end{abstract}
```
